PART 19 - PRIORITY ORDER FOR IMPLEMENTATION

Priority 1 (Implement First) - Core foundation:

library/types.pl (pai_uuid, pai_constant, pai_truth).

library/similarity.pl (pai_similar, pai_dissimilar, pai_closest_match).

library/record.pl basic operations (pai_blueprint, pai_scaffold, forge, recall_or_forge, dissolve, amend, wipe, record_of, query_records).

Priority 2 (Implement Second) - Problem solving and concurrency:

library/problems.pl (pai_challenge, pai_deliberate, state types).

library/tasks.pl (pai_task, pai_async, pai_sync, pai_await, pai_scatter, pai_critical, pai_ask).

Priority 3 (Implement Third) - Data structures and utilities:

library/collections.pl, library/generators.pl, library/config.pl.

Priority 4 (Implement Fourth) - Communication and extension:

library/peers.pl, library/macros.pl, library/convenience.pl.

Priority 5 (Implement Last) - Advanced features:

Extended query_records with inheritance-aware matching.

lattice_dump/2 export functionality.

pai_closest_records/4 similarity-based record queries.

In parallel with Priority 1, run the PR 2 vector backend bake-off, since its outcome shapes PR 3, PR 11, and PR 16; and stand up the PR 1 launcher early, since every demonstration and test benefits from the single prologai entry point.


PART 20 - UPSTREAM CONTRIBUTION STRATEGY

Three PRs from PrologAI are genuinely suitable for upstream SWI-Prolog contribution.

PR 6 (Cyclic Actors) as library(background_worker) - STRONGEST CANDIDATE.

PR 7 (Receptors) as library(message_endpoint) - SECOND STRONGEST.

PR 8 (Subscribe/Publish) as library(pubsub) - THIRD STRONGEST.

All three should be stripped of all PrologAI and cognitive architecture framing before submission.

Recommended sequence: post on the SWI-Prolog Discourse forum at https://swi-prolog.discourse.group first; develop all three as a single pack such as pack(concurrent_agents) or pack(async_prolog); publish and let it mature for six months to a year; then propose core inclusion with community adoption as evidence.

For PR 14's MCP work: contribute improvements to the existing SWI-Prolog MCP Server pack by Peter Trebing rather than creating a competing one.

SWI-Prolog's own guidance is relevant here: implementing additional generic infrastructure that makes your research easier allows you to contribute that infrastructure to SWI-Prolog, makes your code more sustainable, and lets your code reach a wider audience.

The key test for core acceptance: does this feature benefit all or most SWI-Prolog users regardless of what they are building?

Upstream contribution is not a side strategy - it is the second stage of the escalation path defined in Part 31 (library first, then upstream proposal, then a small patch set only as last resort); every accepted contribution is code the project never maintains alone again.


PART 21 - SYSTEM ACCEPTANCE CRITERIA

AC-SYS-001: Given a clean Ubuntu 22.04 system with SWI-Prolog and the Rust toolchain installed, when the PrologAI install script is run, then the prologai executable (launcher plus packs plus compiled prologai-core) is produced without errors in under 30 minutes.

AC-SYS-002: Given the prologai executable, when it is launched, then it displays a startup banner identifying it as PrologAI (with the embedded SWI-Prolog attribution line) and drops to an interactive top-level.

AC-SYS-003: Given the prologai executable, when the query use_module(library(lattice)) is executed, then it succeeds without errors.

AC-SYS-004: Given a PrologAI process with 10,000 node_facts in the Lattice, when traverse_nexus is called with a pattern matching 100 node_facts, then results are returned in under 100 milliseconds.

AC-SYS-005: Given a running PrologAI session, when the query current_prolog_flag(prologai_version, V) is executed, then it succeeds with V = '1.0.0'.

AC-SYS-006: Given a running PrologAI session, when the query current_prolog_flag(dialect, D) is executed, then it succeeds with D = swi.

AC-SYS-007: Given a built PrologAI system, when the query use_module(library(lists)) is executed (a standard SWI-Prolog library), then it succeeds, confirming backward compatibility.

AC-SYS-008: Given a released SWI-Prolog upgrade within the same major version, when the PrologAI packs are reinstalled against it, then the full Level 1 and Level 2 test suites pass without source changes (the no-fork dividend).


PART 22 - PERFORMANCE REQUIREMENTS

NFR-SYS-001: PrologAI startup time shall not exceed 5 seconds on hardware with at least 8 GB RAM and a modern multi-core processor.

NFR-SYS-002: traverse_nexus/4 shall return results in under 100 milliseconds for a Lattice containing up to 100,000 node_facts.

NFR-SYS-003: PrologAI shall be buildable on Linux (Ubuntu 22.04 LTS or later), macOS (12 or later), and Windows (10 or later).

NFR-SYS-004: All PrologAI library modules shall include PlDoc documentation (PlDoc is SWI-Prolog's built-in documentation system) compatible with the SWI-Prolog documentation generator.

NFR-SYS-005: PrologAI shall be licensed under Apache-2.0 for its own original code (see Part 1 Section 1.1), with upstream BSD-2, and MIT OR Apache-2.0 licenses retained for SWI-Prolog and the vector backend components respectively.

NFR-SYS-006: The MCP Gateway shall respond to all requests within 500 milliseconds for operations that do not involve traverse_nexus.


PART 23 - TEST PLANS: FIVE LEVELS

Level 1 - Unit Tests (per actor)

Every individual actor is tested in isolation.

Each cyclic_actor is tested for: correct startup, correct goal execution, correct error recovery, and correct shutdown.

Each receptor is tested for: correct registration, correct message receipt, correct handler invocation, and correct error handling.

Each sentinel is tested for: correct pattern matching (logical), correct semantic routing (Phase 2), correct priority ordering, and correct action execution.

Each Lattice operation is tested for: correct inscription (anchor_node), correct removal (prune_node), correct scanning (traverse_nexus), and correct kindle_node/quench_node behavior.

Level 2 - Integration Tests (per design pattern)

Each of the 11 design patterns is tested as an integrated unit.

The observation pattern integration test: a simulated device sends a sequence of sensory signals, and the test verifies that the correct node_facts are anchored in the Lattice in the correct order with correct values.

The deliberation pattern integration test: the Lattice is preloaded with a situation, objectives, and causal_plans; the test verifies that the deliberation actors select and execute the highest-priority applicable plan.

The motivation pattern integration test: homeostatic variables are driven to extremes and the test verifies that the attention actors generate the correct objectives in priority order.

Level 3 - Scenario Tests (end-to-end use cases)

Full scenarios run from device perception through reasoning to action, testing the complete information flow.

The Community Garden Project scenarios are the primary scenario test suite.

Level 4 - Longitudinal Development Tests

The system is run continuously for an extended period and assessed at intervals against the Piagetian Milestone Battery.

The development trajectory (which milestones are achieved, in what order, at what rate) is compared against theoretical predictions from Piaget's developmental stages.

Level 5 - Architecture Audit (executed during the Refinement SPARC step)

The loops audit: for every actor, trace that its outputs eventually re-influence its own future inputs - no dead-end pipelines, because cognition is settling loops, not a feed-forward pass.

The epistemology audit: check PR 21 evidence truth and PR 29 coherence against formal-epistemology desiderata (calibration and convergence on stable evidence streams), with findings filed as specification defects.


PART 24 - DETAILED TEST SCRIPTS

Test Script 1 - Object Permanence

Setup: manifest_body a simulated device with a camera sensor.

Step 1: relay_percept a visual percept of Object_A at position (3, 4).

Step 2: Verify the Lattice contains a node_fact for Object_A at that position.

Step 3: relay_percept a visual percept of Object_A moving to position (5, 6).

Step 4: Verify kindle_node has updated Object_A's position node_fact.

Step 5: relay_percept a visual percept with Object_A absent (occluded).

Step 6: Verify Object_A's node_fact is NOT pruned from the Lattice; it is quenched but retained.

Step 7: relay_percept a visual percept of Object_A reappearing at position (5, 6).

Step 8: Verify Object_A's node_fact is re-kindled at the correct position.

Pass criteria: Object_A persists in the Lattice throughout occlusion, with correct position retained.

Test Script 2 - Goal Pursuit Under Constraint

Setup: Initialize apex_mind with a motivation sentinel monitoring a battery_level homeostatic variable.

Step 1: Set battery_level actual value to 20 percent (well below target of 80 percent).

Step 2: Verify that the motivation actor inscribes an objective: restore(battery_level, 80.0).

Step 3: Verify that the deliberation actors select a causal_plan: find_power_source.

Step 4: relay_percept a signal indicating the charging station is blocked.

Step 5: Verify that the compensation actor is triggered and generates an alternative plan.

Step 6: relay_percept a signal indicating charging completed.

Step 7: Verify that battery_level node_fact is updated and the objective is resolved.

Pass criteria: system generates, pursues, adapts, and closes a goal cycle correctly.

Test Script 3 - Theory of Mind (Sally-Anne Variant)

Setup: Initialize apex_mind with ontological scopes for Agent_Alice and Agent_Bob.

Step 1: relay_percept that Object_Marble was placed in Location_Basket by Agent_Alice.

Step 2: Verify scope for Agent_Alice contains a belief node_fact reflecting that Agent_Alice believes Object_Marble is in Location_Basket.

Step 3: relay_percept that Agent_Alice exits the scene.

Step 4: relay_percept that Agent_Bob moves Object_Marble from Location_Basket to Location_Box.

Step 5: Verify the system's present_zone scope reflects location(Object_Marble, Location_Box).

Step 6: Query: where_will_seek(Agent_Alice, Object_Marble, Where)?

Step 7: Verify response: Where = Location_Basket.

Pass criteria: system maintains distinct scopes for different agents and correctly reasons from Agent_Alice's perspective.

Test Script 4 - Causal Learning via Regulation

Setup: Initialize apex_mind with a regulation sentinel monitoring attempt outcomes.

Step 1: Execute a causal_plan: water_plants.

Step 2: relay_percept a proprioceptive signal that water_level_in_garden dropped to 0 (plan failed).

Step 3: Verify that the verifier fires and identifies a mismatch between expected and actual outcome.

Step 4: Verify that the regulation actor modifies the water_plants causal_plan (reduces its reliability score) and inscribes a plan_needs_revision node_fact.

Step 5: Execute the modified plan: water_plants_with_check.

Step 6: relay_percept that water_level_in_garden = target value (plan succeeded).

Step 7: Verify that the regulation actor increases the water_plants_with_check plan's reliability score.

Pass criteria: system learns from failure, modifies behavior, and reinforces improved behavior.

Test Script 5 - Episodic Recall via ReasoningBank

Setup: Run the system through 10 episodes of the Community Garden Project scenario.

Step 1: Submit each completed episode trajectory to sona_absorb/1.

Step 2: Present a new situation matching an episode from 3 rounds ago.

Step 3: Call sona_retrieve/2 with the current situation pattern.

Step 4: Verify that the most similar past trajectory is returned.

Step 5: Verify that the recalled trajectory's action sequence is used as a starting point for the current plan.

Pass criteria: system correctly recalls the most relevant past episode and uses it for current planning.


PART 25 - BENCHMARK TARGETS

Benchmark 1 - ARC-AGI and ARC-AGI-2 (Abstraction and Reasoning Corpus for Artificial General Intelligence)

Created by Francois Chollet, ARC-AGI tests fluid intelligence through visual pattern puzzles requiring abstraction from a handful of examples; the harder successor ARC-AGI-2 (2025) and the associated ARC Prize are the current frontier of this family, on which late-2025 frontier systems still score well below skilled humans, making it a live measure of genuine generalization rather than memorization.

PrologAI's inductive reasoning mechanism (PR 37) and the dynamic interlaced hierarchy organization of the Lattice are directly suited to this, and ARC-AGI-2 is adopted as a standing target for the induction and composition layer.

Benchmark 2 - Winograd Schema Challenge

Tests commonsense reasoning and pronoun resolution requiring world knowledge.

PrologAI's ontological memory with its causal and analogical inference mechanisms is designed to perform this kind of commonsense inference.

Benchmark 3 - Theory of Mind Tests (ToMi, HiToM)

Theory of mind (definition) is the ability to attribute mental states to others and understand that others' mental states may differ from one's own.

PrologAI's scope system and its ontological memory of agents with distinct belief states directly implement the mechanisms needed to pass theory of mind tests.

Benchmark 4 - ARC (AI2 Reasoning Challenge)

Grade-school science questions requiring multi-step reasoning and causal understanding.

Benchmark 5 - BIG-bench (Beyond the Imitation Game Benchmark)

Over 200 tasks probing capabilities including logical reasoning, causal inference, mathematical reasoning, and creative problem solving.

Benchmark 6 - GAIA (General AI Assistants Benchmark)

Tests multi-step reasoning, tool use, web research, and file handling.

Benchmark 7 - Piagetian Milestone Battery (custom PrologAI benchmark)

Stage 1: reflex coordination.

Stage 2: object permanence.

Stage 3: goal-directed behavior.

Stage 4: deferred imitation.

Stage 5: symbolic representation.

Stage 6: conservation.

Stage 7: theory of mind.

Stage 8: formal operations.

RSA-TCF Evaluation Benchmarks (research-track, see Part 31 Flag 3):

R1 - Metacognitive Accuracy: measures how accurately the system predicts its own performance before attempting a task.

R2 - Temporal Coherence Score: measures consistency of assertions over configurable time windows.

R3 - Self-Improvement Gain: measures performance improvement on held-out tasks after one RSI cycle.

R4 - Abstraction Transfer: measures learning strategy transfer across domains.


PART 26 - REAL-WORLD DATASETS FOR TESTING

Dataset 1 - UCI Machine Learning Repository (https://archive.ics.uci.edu)

Relevant datasets: Iris Dataset (inductive concept formation test), Adult Income Dataset (analogical and inductive reasoning), Car Evaluation Dataset (categorical hierarchy learning), Balance Scale Dataset (causal reasoning), Mushroom Dataset (safety-critical decision making under uncertainty).

Recommended test approach: present each dataset to PrologAI as a sequence of percepts (one record at a time), allow it to form its own ontological categories, and test its classification accuracy against the dataset labels.

Dataset 2 - Google Dataset Search (https://datasetsearch.research.google.com)

For longitudinal behavioral datasets, multi-agent coordination datasets, environmental sensor time series, and text plus action sequence datasets.

Dataset 3 - Data.gov (https://data.gov)

Relevant: NOAA (National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration) Climate Data for the Community Garden Project weather simulation; USDA (United States Department of Agriculture) National Agricultural Statistics for agricultural planning causal models; CDC (Centers for Disease Control and Prevention) Health Surveillance Data for pattern recognition and prediction; EPA (Environmental Protection Agency) Environmental Monitoring Data for sensor stream scenarios.

Dataset 4 - World Bank Open Data (https://data.worldbank.org)

World Development Indicators for long-horizon temporal reasoning and causal inference about economic dynamics.

Dataset 5 - AWS Open Data (https://registry.opendata.aws)

Relevant: OpenStreetMap for spatial reasoning and navigation planning, NASA Earth Exchange for satellite imagery and environmental prediction, Common Crawl for natural language percept processing at scale.

Dataset 6 - OpenML (https://www.openml.org)

For standardized task definitions with supervised ML baselines for comparison.

OpenML is particularly valuable because it allows direct performance comparison of PrologAI against hundreds of established machine learning baselines.

Dataset 7 - Hugging Face Datasets (https://huggingface.co/datasets)

Relevant: CommonsenseQA, HellaSwag, ARC Easy and Challenge, Social IQa, ToMi (Theory of Mind benchmark), BoolQ.

Dataset 8 - FiveThirtyEight (https://data.fivethirtyeight.com)

Sports, election, and economic indicator data with expert human causal explanations for Turing-style evaluation comparison.


PART 27 - ONTOLOGY DATASETS FOR TESTING

Ontology Dataset 1 - Wikidata (https://www.wikidata.org)

Over 100 million entity items with structured claims.

Ideal for bootstrapping the Lattice with a rich initial knowledge base.

Specific test: load a subset of Wikidata (e.g., all entities and relations related to plants) and test whether the coordination actors can correctly answer queries that require traversing the knowledge graph.

Ontology Dataset 2 - ConceptNet (https://conceptnet.io)

Over 34 million relations across 8 million concepts in 83 languages.

ConceptNet's relations (IsA, PartOf, UsedFor, CapableOf, HasProperty, Causes, MotivatedByGoal) map directly onto PrologAI's bootstrap schema relation types.

Specific test: load ConceptNet English relations and test commonsense reasoning performance on the Winograd Schema Challenge and CommonsenseQA benchmarks.

Ontology Dataset 3 - WordNet (https://wordnet.princeton.edu)

Princeton's large English lexical database organizing words into synsets interlinked by conceptual-semantic relations.

WordNet's synset hierarchy maps directly onto PrologAI's genus relation (type-of relationships in the Lattice).

Ontology Dataset 4 - DBpedia (https://www.dbpedia.org)

Large, richly interconnected knowledge graph of real-world entities extracted from Wikipedia.

Particularly useful for testing PrologAI's ability to integrate encyclopedic world knowledge with dynamically perceived and learned knowledge.

Ontology Dataset 5 - Gene Ontology (https://geneontology.org)

One of the largest and most rigorously structured scientific ontologies.

Tests PrologAI's ability to reason over deep expert-level taxonomies far outside common sense.

Ontology Dataset 6 - OBO Foundry (https://obofoundry.org)

Over 150 specialized biomedical ontologies.

The multi-ontology nature directly tests PrologAI's scope system: different ontologies can be loaded into different scopes, and the system must correctly navigate between scopes to answer cross-domain queries.


PART 28 - THE COMMUNITY GARDEN PROJECT: PRIMARY INTEGRATION TEST ENVIRONMENT

Setting: A 50-plot community garden in a mid-sized city over multiple simulated seasons.

The garden has: 50 individual plot holders, a garden manager (the PrologAI mind), a shared tool shed, a shared water source with metered usage, a composting system, a seed library, seasonal planting schedules, and a community bulletin board.

The world has: four seasons with distinct planting and maintenance requirements, weekly weather events from a Markov chain weather model (states: sunny, cloudy, rainy, frost, drought, storm), monthly volunteer coordination events, annual harvest festivals, and unexpected events such as pest infestations, equipment breakdowns, and new plot holder arrivals.

Time flows at 1 simulated day per 10 real seconds (one simulated year approximately equals 1 real hour).

Sensor streams transmitted as percepts to PrologAI:

Signal type 1 - Soil moisture: perception_signal(soil_moisture, moisture_reading(PlotId, Value), Timestamp).

Signal type 2 - Temperature: perception_signal(temperature, temp_reading(Celsius), Timestamp).

Signal type 3 - Weather event: perception_signal(weather, weather_event(Type), Timestamp).

Signal type 4 - Plot occupancy: perception_signal(plot_update, plot_event(PlotId, MemberId, Action, CropType), Timestamp).

Signal type 5 - Resource levels: interoception_signal(water_supply, CurrentLevel, Timestamp) and interoception_signal(tool_availability, AvailableCount, Timestamp).

Commands issued by PrologAI:

dispatch_command(Body, water_plot(PlotId, AmountLiters)).

dispatch_command(Body, send_frost_alert(MemberIdList)).

dispatch_command(Body, request_volunteers(Date, TaskDescription)).

dispatch_command(Body, recommend_planting(PlotId, CropType, Date)).

Cognitive requirements from PrologAI during the scenario:

CR-CGP-001: PrologAI shall form a causal model of crop growth predicting yield given soil moisture, temperature, and sunlight.

CR-CGP-002: PrologAI shall maintain a scope for each plot holder representing their known preferences and reliability history.

CR-CGP-003: PrologAI shall generate a seasonal planting plan before each season using daydream_actor simulation of at least 3 alternatives.

CR-CGP-004: PrologAI shall detect frost events within 1 simulated day of occurrence and immediately issue frost alerts.

CR-CGP-005: PrologAI shall learn from crop failures: after 3 consecutive failures of the same crop in the same plot, inscribe a constraint node_fact avoiding that crop-plot combination.

CR-CGP-006: PrologAI shall track water resource levels and implement rationing when water_supply drops below 30 percent of capacity.

Scenario success criteria:

SC-CGP-001: After 1 simulated year, PrologAI's average crop yield prediction accuracy shall be at least 70 percent.

SC-CGP-002: After 2 simulated years, PrologAI's planting recommendations shall produce at least 20 percent higher average yields than a random baseline.

SC-CGP-003: PrologAI shall achieve Piagetian Milestone Level 3 (goal-directed behavior) by the end of simulated year 1.

SC-CGP-004: PrologAI shall achieve Piagetian Milestone Level 5 (symbolic representation) by the end of simulated year 2.

SC-CGP-005: PrologAI shall detect and respond to 100 percent of frost events within the required time window.

All 11 design patterns are exercised simultaneously in this single coherent scenario.

Dataset structure: garden_state, weather_log, plot_holder_log, resource_log, event_log, and outcome_log files.


PART 29 - PRESENTING PROLOGAI TO SOCIETY

Audience 1 - The Scientific and Technical Community

PrologAI is presented through peer-reviewed papers demonstrating: the architecture's theoretical foundations in Piagetian developmental theory and cognitive design patterns; empirical results from developmental assessments; benchmark performance; and ablation study findings.

Target venues include: the AGI Conference, the Cognitive Science Society annual conference, the AAAI Conference on Artificial Intelligence, the IEEE International Conference on Development and Learning (ICDL), and the Biologically Inspired Cognitive Architectures (BICA) symposium.

Audience 2 - The Developer and Technology Community

PrologAI is presented as an open-source cognitive architecture framework with clear documentation, tutorials, and example implementations.

The GitHub repository is the primary distribution channel.

SWI-Prolog's pack registry distributes the general-purpose components (background workers, message endpoints, pubsub library).

Audience 3 - The General Public

Key messages:

This system learns the way a child learns - from experience, not from being programmed.

It sets its own goals based on its own needs.

It remembers past experiences and uses them to make better decisions.

It regulates its own behavior and improves over time.

It is not a chatbot - it does not just answer questions.

It is a system that thinks, plans, acts, and develops.

The Community Garden Project serves as a relatable, concrete illustration of what a mind built with PrologAI does in practice.


PART 30 - SUMMARY OF COMMANDS TO GET STARTED TODAY

There is no fork; getting started means installing the engine, creating the platform repositories, and building the first pack.

Step 1: Install SWI-Prolog (9.x or later).

On Ubuntu: sudo apt install swi-prolog

On macOS: brew install swi-prolog

On Windows: download the installer from https://www.swi-prolog.org/Download.html

Step 2: Install the Rust toolchain (for prologai-core).

curl --proto '=https' --tlsv1.2 -sSf https://sh.rustup.rs | sh

Step 3: Create and clone the platform monorepo.

git clone git@github.com:YOUR_ORG/prologai.git
cd prologai

Step 4: Create the first pack skeleton (the lattice pack).

mkdir -p packs/lattice/prolog packs/lattice/test
(create packs/lattice/pack.pl with name, version, title, author, and requires fields per the SWI-Prolog pack documentation)

Step 5: Create and build the Rust crate repository.

cd ..
git clone git@github.com:YOUR_ORG/prologai-core.git
cd prologai-core
cargo build --release

Step 6: Clone the candidate vector backend for the PR 2 bake-off (evaluation only at this stage).

cd ..
git clone https://github.com/ruvnet/RuVector.git

Step 7: Link the packs for development.

swipl -g "pack_install('./prologai/packs/lattice', [link(true)])" -t halt

Step 8: Run the test suite.

swipl -g "use_module(library(lattice)), run_tests" -t halt

Step 9: Begin PR 1 (the launcher) and PR 2 (the FFI bridge and bake-off) on feature branches of the monorepo.

git checkout -b feature/pr01-launcher

Each subsequent feature (Lattice, node_facts, cyclic actors, receptors, sentinels, subscribe and publish, mind-body interface, SONA learning, assessment, reflection actors, MCP gateway, tabling truth maintenance, embedding provider) follows the same pattern: create a branch, write the code and tests, commit, push, and open a PR against the monorepo.

You now have the engine installed, both PrologAI repositories on your local machine, the candidate backend cloned for evaluation, and a PR workflow in place - with nothing forked.

For a slower, command-by-command walkthrough of everything above, including the answer to "but should we not be forking swipl-devel?", see the micro-step guides in Part 32.


PART 31 - ARCHITECTURE DECISION RECORD ADR-1: LAYERED PLATFORM, NOT FORK

An Architecture Decision Record, or ADR (definition), is a short document that captures an important architectural decision, the context in which it was made, the alternatives considered, and its consequences, so that future contributors understand not just what was decided but why.

This ADR integrates two analysis documents: the PrologAI Build Approach Assessment and the Constructed Programming Language (CPL) Base Decision, both of which were supported by live web research.


SECTION 31.1 - CONTEXT

PrologAI requires a Constructed Programming Language (CPL) that will enable the creation of an Artificial Mind (AMIND), also called a Synthetic Mind (SMIND).

The delivery vehicle was re-examined from first principles before implementation began: should the CPL be built (A) on SWI-Prolog, (B) on some other free open source language, or (C) from scratch?


SECTION 31.2 - THE DECISION

Decision 1: Option A - SWI-Prolog is the base.

Decision 2: "Base" means engine, not property - PrologAI is a true constructed language with its own name, executable, surface syntax, and manual, which compiles to and runs on an UNFORKED SWI-Prolog, the way Elixir rides the unforked Erlang virtual machine, the way Clojure rides the unforked Java Virtual Machine, and the way Logtalk has ridden standard Prolog backends as a transcompiler for over twenty-five years.

Decision 3: Delivery is in three stages - Stage 1: the cognitive platform as SWI-Prolog packs plus the prologai-core Rust crate (PRs 2 through 16); Stage 2: the language proper - launcher, dialect flags, and .pai transcompiler (PR 1); Stage 3: pushing primitives down into Rust or upstream into SWI-Prolog only when profiling proves the library layer insufficient.

Decision 4: A whole-engine fork is held in reserve as a last resort with a written trigger condition: a measured, reproducible engine-level bottleneck that cannot be solved in prologai-core and that the SWI-Prolog maintainers decline to address; even then, the first response is a small, documented patch set, never a renamed whole-repository fork.


SECTION 31.3 - WHY NOT A FORK (THE HONEST ASSESSMENT)

Reason 1 - The specification itself proves the fork unnecessary.

Of the fourteen original work packages, thirteen never touch the SWI-Prolog virtual machine: PR 2 is a foreign library loaded with use_foreign_library/1; PRs 3, 4, and 5 load via use_module; PR 6's own implementation sketch is six lines of plain Prolog over the built-in thread_create/3; PR 7 is built on the built-in message queues; PRs 8 through 14 are pure Prolog modules layered above.

Only PR 1 (rebranding) required a fork, and a launcher achieves the same identity without one.

A fork would therefore have bought exactly one thing - a renamed startup banner - at the price of permanent maintenance debt.

Reason 2 - Fork maintenance costs are real and documented.

The swipl-devel repository is large, written in C, uses Git submodules, and receives continuous upstream changes; industry research on private forks finds organizations spending on the order of 60 labor hours per release cycle per fork on rebasing and integration, with conflict pain compounding as local changes accumulate.

Every SWI-Prolog improvement would have arrived in a fork late, painfully, or never; in the layered design, AC-SYS-008 instead verifies that engine upgrades arrive for free.

Reason 3 - A fork forfeits the host's gifts.

SWI-Prolog already provides threads and message queues (the actor substrate), engines designed for massive many-agent concurrency, shared incremental tabling with well-founded semantics (a ready-made truth maintenance system, now exploited by PR 15), transactions, persistence, just-in-time clause indexing, the maintained swipl-rs Rust bridge (used in production by TerminusDB), an HTTP stack, an existing MCP server, and the pack distribution system.

Reason 4 - The reflective architecture is better served unforked.

The mind modifies PrologAI-layer code, which is Prolog data and fully reflectable, while the engine below stays immutable: the layering itself structurally enforces the protected-core and constitutional constraints of Section 3.16.


SECTION 31.4 - WHY NOT OPTION C (FROM SCRATCH)

The timeline evidence is decisive.

Rust, created in 2006 and sponsored by Mozilla from 2009 with a paid compiler team, reached 1.0 in May 2015 - about nine years from creation.

Julia, launched publicly in 2012 by a team of programming language researchers, reached 1.0 in August 2018 - about six years.

MeTTa, the one language built from scratch specifically for Artificial General Intelligence (by the OpenCog Hyperon project, with dedicated funding and a veteran team), remained in maturation toward production readiness after years of effort, per its own late-2025 roadmap.

A from-scratch language means building a parser, compiler or interpreter, garbage collector, module system, debugger, editor integration, package manager, standard library, and documentation - and not one hour of that work advances the Artificial Mind.

The goal is a mind; the language is the means; option C inverts that.


SECTION 31.5 - WHY NOT OPTION B (ANOTHER BASE), CANDIDATE BY CANDIDATE

Candidate B1 - Racket: by design a programming-language programming language (its hash-lang mechanism has hosted dozens of real languages, and it is the world's leading platform for language-oriented programming), but it has no industrial logic engine - its Racklog and Datalog libraries are shallow relative to thirty-five years of Prolog engineering; the right move is to borrow Racket's language-building discipline for the PR 1 transcompiler, not to adopt Racket as the base.

Candidate B2 - Erlang or Elixir on the BEAM virtual machine: the best actor runtime ever built (millions of lightweight processes, supervision trees, hot code reload), but no unification, no backtracking, no clause database - the reasoning core would have to be written from nothing; notably, the first Erlang was itself written in Prolog; the right move is to borrow supervision-tree thinking for the actor framework, not to adopt the BEAM as the base.

Candidate B3 - Common Lisp or Scheme: homoiconic and macro-rich, but no logic engine and no cognitive primitives; strictly dominated by option A for a logic-programming mind design.

Candidate B4 - Other logic languages: Mercury is purely declarative and compiled, but its purity forbids the dynamic, self-modifying fact store this mind is built on, and releases have slowed; Scryer Prolog is a promising Rust Prolog with serious International Organization for Standardization (ISO) ambitions but is pre-1.0 with its package system still being planned - a future second backend for the dialect layer, not today's base; Oz/Mozart is effectively dormant; Curry is a research language; XSB pioneered tabling, which SWI-Prolog has since absorbed while offering better concurrency and tooling.

Candidate B5 - Rust as the base: the right implementation language for components (prologai-core), the wrong base for the language semantics - adopting it as the base is option C with a nicer toolbox.

Summary: the best of option B lives inside an A-shaped project - Racket's discipline in the syntax layer, BEAM-style supervision in the actor layer, and Rust in the performance layer.


SECTION 31.6 - ENGINEERING HONESTY FLAGS

Flag 1 - RuVector requires independent verification.

RuVector exists (github.com/ruvnet/ruvector, MIT OR Apache-2.0) and its feature list matches this specification's needs, but its headline numbers (150 times faster, sub-half-millisecond search, self-learning topology) are the project's own claims with no independent benchmarks found, and its author publishes prolifically with heavy use of AI-generated code - a style the practitioner literature characterizes as fast but flawed and often light on review.

Unproven does not mean bad; it means untested.

Disposition: PR 2 defines a backend-agnostic interface and a measured bake-off against established alternatives (hnswlib, usearch, FAISS); the winner becomes the default; the dependency stays swappable forever.

Flag 2 - Hash-based embeddings are not semantic.

A deterministic hash-based projection preserves identity, not meaning: apple and pear land no closer than apple and carburetor.

Disposition: PR 16 introduces the pluggable embedding provider; the hash method is retained but relabeled structural; genuine semantic similarity requires a learned embedding model, local by default.

Flag 3 - The advanced self-modeling material is research-track.

The RSA-TCF framework, ASI primitives, coherence energy functionals, and Bayes-optimal convergence claims are research aspirations whose quantities are not yet falsifiable; they are preserved in this specification (Section 3.17, Part 25 R-benchmarks) but explicitly designated research-track: they do not gate, block, or distort the buildable core, which is PRs 1 through 16.


SECTION 31.7 - CONSEQUENCES

Positive: months-faster first working mind; permanent free inheritance of engine improvements; full SWI-Prolog ecosystem compatibility; upstream contribution path (Part 20) becomes a first-class strategy; the constructed language remains retargetable because it is defined by this written specification rather than by an implementation.

Negative, accepted: PrologAI's identity must be earned through its launcher, syntax, documentation, and results rather than through ownership of the engine's repository; some performance ceilings are set by the engine until Stage 3 escalation; the bake-off adds early work before the memory layer is final.

Revisit condition: this ADR is revisited if (a) the Stage 3 trigger fires, (b) Scryer Prolog reaches maturity sufficient to serve as a second backend and portability becomes strategic, or (c) the mind itself, at developmental stage formal_operations with constitutional permits, proposes and validates an engine-level improvement worth carrying as a patch set.


SECTION 31.8 - SOURCES SUPPORTING THIS ADR (PLAIN ADDRESSES)

SWI-Prolog packs and extension documentation: https://www.swi-prolog.org/howto/Pack.html

SWI-Prolog features (threads, engines, tabling): https://www.swi-prolog.org/features.html

SWI-Prolog engines for many-agent applications: https://www.swi-prolog.org/pldoc/man?section=engine-agents

SWI-Prolog tabling for changing world models: https://www.swi-prolog.org/blog/tabling-for-blackboard.md

SWI-Prolog main development repository: https://github.com/SWI-Prolog/swipl-devel

swipl-rs Rust bindings (TerminusDB Labs): https://github.com/terminusdb-labs/swipl-rs

RuVector repository and claims: https://github.com/ruvnet/ruvector

Logtalk, a language layered on Prolog backends: https://logtalk.org/

Racket language-oriented programming: https://cacm.acm.org/practice/creating-languages-in-racket/

Rust timeline to 1.0: https://blog.rust-lang.org/2015/02/13/Final-1.0-timeline/

Julia timeline to 1.0: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julia_(programming_language)

OpenCog Hyperon and MeTTa: https://hyperon.opencog.org/
